  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_81bf3925-5619-4a34-9e5c-d85cf961deea'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);
    };

      initAppleId();

      initializeSiwa();

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer4495220898131831166"><p>He aquí la creación más lograda y de carácter más universal de Calderón. <i>La vida es sueño</i> es, en síntesis, la plasmación barroca de la idea de la fugacidad de la vida con todos los aditamentos geniales de construcción, caracteres y estilo que el autor supo imprimirle. Con este pesimismo radical sobre el valor de la vida humana se interfiere el libre albedrío como afirmació</p></span>
  <span id="freeText4495220898131831166" style="display:none"><p>He aquí la creación más lograda y de carácter más universal de Calderón. <i>La vida es sueño</i> es, en síntesis, la plasmación barroca de la idea de la fugacidad de la vida con todos los aditamentos geniales de construcción, caracteres y estilo que el autor supo imprimirle. Con este pesimismo radical sobre el valor de la vida humana se interfiere el libre albedrío como afirmación personal de Segismundo —“¿y teniendo yo más vida / tengo menos libertad?”—. Estos dos principios combinados crean una riqueza enorme de sentidos, que en esta edición son desmenuzados críticamente por Ciriaco Morón Arroyo.</p></span>
  <a data-text-id="4495220898131831166" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_37308824" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/37308824" />
    <a title="supernal" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1101509-supernal"><img alt="supernal" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1211972350p2/1101509.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/37308824?book_show_action=true">Nov 10, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="supernal" class="user" itemprop="url" name="supernal" href="/user/show/1101509-supernal">supernal</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780143104827" href="/book/show/42213.Life_Is_a_Dream">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer37308824" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14662442927882615965">This book has to be the most amazing play that I've ever read. There are those that say that Pedro Calderon is second only to the great <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/18135.Romeo_and_Juliet' title='Romeo and Juliet by William Shakespeare' rel='nofollow noopener'>William Shakespeare</a>, in terms of playwright, but I have to say that I think that Pedro's writing at the very least matches Shakespeare.<br/><br/>Don't get me wrong. When properly translated, Shakespearian writing can have a profound and powerful effect the likes of which are scarcely recreated in contemporary writing, but I feel the same can be said of Pedro Calderon.<br/><br/>I re</span>
  <span id="freeText14662442927882615965" style="display:none">This book has to be the most amazing play that I've ever read. There are those that say that Pedro Calderon is second only to the great <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/18135.Romeo_and_Juliet" title="Romeo and Juliet by William Shakespeare" rel="nofollow noopener">William Shakespeare</a>, in terms of playwright, but I have to say that I think that Pedro's writing at the very least matches Shakespeare.<br /><br />Don't get me wrong. When properly translated, Shakespearian writing can have a profound and powerful effect the likes of which are scarcely recreated in contemporary writing, but I feel the same can be said of Pedro Calderon.<br /><br />I recommend this book to anyone who has wondered about the substance of their existence, about what makes their reality more concrete than any other, or who just enjoys intense fits of philosophy inspired by intense bits of text.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14662442927882615965" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3453375045" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17247950539374783792">Life Is a Dream is the most beautiful play I have ever read (and I have read quite a few). Pedro Calderon de la Barca does magic here. I love Shakespeare, Lope de Vega and Ibsen (also Beckett, Shaw and Pirandello to name just few) but Life Is a Dream is the start of everything they wrote. And, I think, the best start that could be.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_135393449" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/135393449" />
    <a title="Miquixote" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4671027-miquixote"><img alt="Miquixote" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1293721764p2/4671027.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/135393449?book_show_action=true">Dec 16, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Miquixote" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Miquixote" href="/user/show/4671027-miquixote">Miquixote</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4671027-miquixote?shelf=spanish">spanish</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4671027-miquixote?shelf=poetry">poetry</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4671027-miquixote?shelf=plays">plays</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4671027-miquixote?shelf=0-8">0-8</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4671027-miquixote?shelf=drama">drama</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4671027-miquixote?shelf=set-in-spain">set-in-spain</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer135393449" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11575231974142786502">A metaphor for authority and oppression. Is authority justified in oppressing us because it believes we would bring disaster if empowered?<br/><br/>Lying about our weakness authority manages to keep us unaware until it betrays what little it has left of humanity and gives us a degree of freedom. When we experience that freedom, this discovery of our limited freedoms make us violent, and we rage.<br/><br/>Horrified and certain of the truthfulness of their fears, authority has us drugged and returned to our prisons. </span>
  <span id="freeText11575231974142786502" style="display:none">A metaphor for authority and oppression. Is authority justified in oppressing us because it believes we would bring disaster if empowered?<br /><br />Lying about our weakness authority manages to keep us unaware until it betrays what little it has left of humanity and gives us a degree of freedom. When we experience that freedom, this discovery of our limited freedoms make us violent, and we rage.<br /><br />Horrified and certain of the truthfulness of their fears, authority has us drugged and returned to our prisons. We mourn, believing everything to be nothing more than a spectacle.<br /><br />Though we remain oblivious in our cells, some have really discovered our plight and would like to break us out of prison (the revolutionaries). Though we will probably rejoice if this happens, we cannot be sure whether this new development would be, in fact, reality or still just another spectacle.<br /><br />The rebels persist, though, and raise an army of the people. Together the rebels defeat the authority, but the people still need their chosen leader. The newly ‘liberated’ leader decides not to take the old authority’s life. <br /><br />The book (naively?) presumes that the authority would be moved at this gesture, and proclaim the son heir. The chosen resolves to live by acknowledging that one must strive for goodness in both dream and reality.<br /><br />A book that reveals a need for the archetypal hero who fights for freedom, in this case a quixotic character unsure of what is real and what is not, a romantic appeal to a non-violent revolution freeing us from an over-emphasis on mystification, opening up a new degree of freedom, that of reality. <br /><br />But allowing us still the freedom that is given by the spirit of the myth, the ideal, the archetype, the unconscious, the immaterial, the imaginatory, the romantic, the spectacle and ultimately art -in all its glory.<br /><br />Goodness in reality and in dream. The creation of a dialectic between dream and reality, between the unconscious and the conscious through the gain of social power. One is only left to wonder if social inclusion will follow from this new leader or will it simply be a new authority?<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11575231974142786502" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22110057-debbie-zapata?shelf=gutenberg">gutenberg</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1616806838" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer729070504867100818">This play was first published in the 1600's in Spain. The translation I read at Project Gutenberg was published in 1873. The introduction by the translator included this information:<br/><br/><i>A note by Hartzenbusch in the last edition of the drama published at Madrid (1872), tells that "La Vida es Sueno", is founded on a story which turns out to be substantially the same as that with which English students are familiar as the foundation of the famous Induction to the "Taming of the Shrew". Calderon found </i></span>
  <span id="freeText729070504867100818" style="display:none">This play was first published in the 1600's in Spain. The translation I read at Project Gutenberg was published in 1873. The introduction by the translator included this information:<br /><br /><i>A note by Hartzenbusch in the last edition of the drama published at Madrid (1872), tells that "La Vida es Sueno", is founded on a story which turns out to be substantially the same as that with which English students are familiar as the foundation of the famous Induction to the "Taming of the Shrew". Calderon found it however in a different work from that in which Shakespeare met with it, or rather his predecessor, the anonymous author of "The Taming of a Shrew", whose work supplied to Shakespeare the materials of his own comedy.</i><br /><br />I have seen a movie version of The Taming Of The Shrew (who could forget Richard Burton and Elizabeth Taylor?) but I don't remember ever reading the play itself. It has been added to my Someday list now, though. I will want to read that 'famous Induction' for myself!<br /><br />Meanwhile, back in Spain....no, back in Poland, since that is where our action takes place....we are introduced to Rosaura, who is a young lady masquerading as a man in order to get revenge for something which we do not learn until later. She and her companion Clarin (the requisite clownish servant) stumble across the hidden tower where Prince Sigismund has been chained his entire life. They are discovered by the nobleman Clotaldo, who takes them to the castle to turn them over to the King. <br /><br />But there is a lot going on beneath the surface: Clotaldo seems to recognize Rosaura, but why? Why does the King, who is considered so wise, rely so much on astrology, and what troubles has that caused him and his country? Why is Prince Sigismund a prisoner in the first place? And the dream? Just where does that come into the plot? Does everyone get their heart's desire by the final curtain? Or is it all just a dream?<br /><br />This was a fun little romp, with some lovely phrases throughout, and a profound message about life<br />and how to live it tossed out bit by bit in the final act. I enjoyed the play, but I would like to read it in the original Spanish someday if possible. There are some speeches with puns and wordplay in them that would sound much better in Spanish. The translator completely changed a few lines in the last act because he did not like the original puns, which made sense in Spanish but not in English. This can be a problem with Spanish, especially when it is spoken, because many words can be used in puns. (The translator added a footnote explaining what he did and why.)<br /><br />Calderon is another new author to me, but I want to explore more of his work. I quote the wiki article: <i>Pedro Calderón de la Barca y Barreda González de Henao Ruiz de Blasco y Riaño, usually referred as Pedro Calderón de la Barca . . . ; (17 January 1600 – 25 May 1681), was a dramatist, poet and writer of the Spanish Golden Age. During certain periods of his life he was also a soldier and a Roman Catholic priest. Born when the Spanish Golden Age theatre was being defined by Lope de Vega, he developed it further, his work being regarded as the culmination of the Spanish Baroque theatre. As such, he is regarded as one of Spain's foremost dramatists and one of the finest playwrights of world literature.</i> <br /><br />Sigismund:<br /><b>In this world's uncertain gleam,<br />That to live is but to dream:<br />Man dreams what he is, and wakes<br />Only when upon him breaks<br />Death's mysterious morning beam.</b><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="729070504867100818" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer5245738247706969027">I'm not usually so much impressed by theatre plays, but this one,this one was very good. I read it with so much curiosity and the plot is really interesting. It was the first Spanish theatre play I've read and I didn't expect it to impress me so much. Only Shakespeare and Dryden have done this to me before xD. When they released Segismundo and after having commited murder and threats and took him back in the tower, at that point I prefigured what will happen. I already imagined that he will real</span>
  <span id="freeText5245738247706969027" style="display:none">I'm not usually so much impressed by theatre plays, but this one,this one was very good. I read it with so much curiosity and the plot is really interesting. It was the first Spanish theatre play I've read and I didn't expect it to impress me so much. Only Shakespeare and Dryden have done this to me before xD. When they released Segismundo and after having commited murder and threats and took him back in the tower, at that point I prefigured what will happen. I already imagined that he will realise how bad he acted and that he will come on the right path.<br />What I enjoyed the most was ,of course, the changing of heart of the principal character, the theme (!) about life being a dream (F.A.Q. by many of us, I suppose, at some point in life) and the verses! So so beautiful verses, full of meaning and teaching about life. Interesting how he looked innocent in the first act, then totally ravishing in the second, and in the last act ... surprisingly good.I regret so much not having read it in Spanish, but I will, one day. When I won't be so much pressed about time. <br /></span>

<div id="review_3070331737" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3070331737" />
    <a title="Roy Lotz" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/14046996-roy-lotz"><img alt="Roy Lotz" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1443324405p2/14046996.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3070331737?book_show_action=true">Dec 02, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Roy Lotz" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Roy Lotz" href="/user/show/14046996-roy-lotz">Roy Lotz</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14046996-roy-lotz?shelf=drama">drama</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14046996-roy-lotz?shelf=best-words-best-order">best-words-best-order</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14046996-roy-lotz?shelf=no-hablo-ingl%C3%A9s">no-hablo-inglés</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14046996-roy-lotz?shelf=hispanophilia">hispanophilia</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3070331737" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2152704504502113461"><blockquote>
  <i>Sueña el rico en su riqueza,<br/>que más cuidados le ofrece;<br/>sueña el pobre que padece<br/>su miseria y su pobreza</i>
</blockquote><br/>Here is another classic play from Spain’s Golden Age. Calderón de la Barca is sometimes considered to be Lope de Vega’s successor—a man who took Spanish theater in the direction of refinement and sophistication. Lope de Vega’s plays, for all of their formal accoutrements, are rather simple human dramas; while Calderón was attracted to more philosophical themes. This play, his most famous, i</span>
  <span id="freeText2152704504502113461" style="display:none"><blockquote>
  <i>Sueña el rico en su riqueza,<br />que más cuidados le ofrece;<br />sueña el pobre que padece<br />su miseria y su pobreza</i>
</blockquote><br />Here is another classic play from Spain’s Golden Age. Calderón de la Barca is sometimes considered to be Lope de Vega’s successor—a man who took Spanish theater in the direction of refinement and sophistication. Lope de Vega’s plays, for all of their formal accoutrements, are rather simple human dramas; while Calderón was attracted to more philosophical themes. This play, his most famous, is exemplary in this respect. Its very title invites speculation. Certainly it is difficult to imagine Lope de Vega writing such a title.<br /><br />Yet on the surface at least, this play does not seem so frightfully abstract. We have two interconnected stories. In the first, a king overly concerned with astrology and prognostication decides that his son was born under a bad star and has the boy thrown into prison, where he grows into a savage man. Meanwhile, a woman disguised as a man rides into town, looking to revenge herself upon the man who took her “honor.” Soon enough it is revealed that she is the natural daughter of a prominent lord at court.<br /><br />So far, all this could easily be the plot of one of Shakespeare’s late Romances. But Calderón does something rather interesting with the philosophical message. The pivotal moment of the play occurs when Segismundo, the imprisoned son, decides to consider all of life as a kind of dream rather than reality. This quasi-mystical realization leads him to abandon his savage ways and to become a beneficent ruler. Arguably this is not a very logical reaction, since if life is no more real than a dream, then one may as well be monstrous as virtuous. But apparently it gives Segismundo the mental detachment needed to transform himself.<br /><br />Calderón is certainly no Shakespeare when it comes to character development. Segismundo’s transformation is abrupt and difficult to believe. But it does give Calderón a chance to write some beautiful verse on the vanity of the world, such as in Act II, Scene 19. In general I think the quality of verse in this play is quite high, not that I am a particularly adept judge at this sort of thing. I am sure, however, that Spanish is far better suited to rhyming verse than English—partly because there are so many more rhymes available, and partly because of the even rhythm of spoken Spanish. It can be hypnotic to read:<br /><blockquote>¿Qué es la vida? Un frenesí.<br />¿Qué es la vida? Una ilusión,<br />una sombra, una ficción,<br />y el mayor bien es pequeño,<br />que toda la vida es sueño,<br />y los sueños sueños son.</blockquote><br />While perhaps not a work of high drama, it makes for a lovely poem. I hope one day to see it performed.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="2152704504502113461" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer15967492712887906810">I just re-read this play yesterday as an option for the SUNY Sullivan season of 14-15 or in years to come. I remember John Wilson in 1998 telling my class at Cornish that this was as perfect as a play could get and I've read it several times and taught it twice, and I agree. I think the next step is to read it in Spanish to get the sense of the Golden Age scansion and its acting rules, but...alas...I need to be more fluent in Spanish...goals, goals, goals... Anyway, I think this is a play that s</span>
  <span id="freeText15967492712887906810" style="display:none">I just re-read this play yesterday as an option for the SUNY Sullivan season of 14-15 or in years to come. I remember John Wilson in 1998 telling my class at Cornish that this was as perfect as a play could get and I've read it several times and taught it twice, and I agree. I think the next step is to read it in Spanish to get the sense of the Golden Age scansion and its acting rules, but...alas...I need to be more fluent in Spanish...goals, goals, goals... Anyway, I think this is a play that should be produced more because (A) a woman gets to sword fight and it's not because she was nontraditionally cast (B) The philosophy of our lives being just a dream is a bit mind-bending and existential before existential was a movement...again...mind-bending and (C) the dialectic on honor vs. humanity is very engaging, and, surprisingly not boring.<br /><br /></span>

It’s a book I highly recommend for those readers who love to find deep meanings in very vague ideas and concepts and enjoy the elaborate language of the baroque literature.<br/><br/>Spanish version:<br/>Las reflexiones sobre lo efímero y la vida humana que nos plantea La Vida es Sueño son ta</span>
  <span id="freeText15119543519384027301" style="display:none">The reflections about the ephemeral of human life exposed in Life is a Dream is as valid as the time it was written, and that makes the work of Calderon de la Barca in one of the most universal Spanish literature readings. Un libro totalmente recomendable para todos aquellos que quieran encontrar significados profundos en ideas y conceptos muy vagos y que disfruten con una literatura barroca y elaborada. <br /></span>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer666367889" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7060202423095389102">A metaphysical play with hints of Chuang Tsu's butterfly dream. This beautiful Spanish classic questions fate's role in our lives. <br/><br/>Basilio, king of Poland, has his would-be parricidal son imprisoned for life. His Laius-like effort to thwart fate actually creates the situation he wants to avoid. For his part Segismund, the son, learns a valuable lesson while in prison and has a monumental decision to make. Who he is, or is to become, is at stake - does he embrace fate or does free will come in to</span>
  <span id="freeText7060202423095389102" style="display:none">A metaphysical play with hints of Chuang Tsu's butterfly dream. This beautiful Spanish classic questions fate's role in our lives. <br /><br />Basilio, king of Poland, has his would-be parricidal son imprisoned for life. His Laius-like effort to thwart fate actually creates the situation he wants to avoid. For his part Segismund, the son, learns a valuable lesson while in prison and has a monumental decision to make. Who he is, or is to become, is at stake - does he embrace fate or does free will come in to play?<br /></span>

<div id="review_2304078190" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2304078190" />
    <a title="Quiver" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9310150-quiver"><img alt="Quiver" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1489326638p2/9310150.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2304078190?book_show_action=true">Apr 11, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Quiver" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Quiver" href="/user/show/9310150-quiver">Quiver</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/18905406-life-is-a-dream">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9310150-quiver?shelf=a-english">a-english</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9310150-quiver?shelf=in-translation-esp-por">in-translation-esp-por</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9310150-quiver?shelf=p-poetry-plays">p-poetry-plays</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2304078190" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10075334369922515169">I stumbled across this play when looking for quotes relating <em>life </em>and <em>dream</em>. I found it on Gutenberg, read the first few pages, and thereafter I was charmed.<br/><br/>Sigismund is Prince and heir to the throne of Poland. It is prophesied that he will have a violent, cruel nature and is therefore imprisoned by his father, the King, in a tower where he grows up under a caretakers guidance. The play begins when Sigismund is a young man, and the King decides to test Sigismund's nature by letting him into the </span>
  <span id="freeText10075334369922515169" style="display:none">I stumbled across this play when looking for quotes relating <em>life </em>and <em>dream</em>. I found it on Gutenberg, read the first few pages, and thereafter I was charmed.<br /><br />Sigismund is Prince and heir to the throne of Poland. It is prophesied that he will have a violent, cruel nature and is therefore imprisoned by his father, the King, in a tower where he grows up under a caretakers guidance. The play begins when Sigismund is a young man, and the King decides to test Sigismund's nature by letting him into the palace and explaining the situation. The King threatens that if Sigismund does not behave these new luxurious surroundings and his princely heritage will all turn out to be but a dream.<br /><br />Drama ensues.<br /><blockquote>What is life? 'Tis but a madness.<br />What is life? A thing that seems,<br />A mirage that falsely gleams,<br />Phantom joy, delusive rest,<br />Since is life a dream at best,<br />And even dreams themselves are dreams.<br /><br />(Sigismund's soliloquy)</blockquote><br /><br />Certain themes stood out:<br /> <br /> 	 Most obvious and tied to the title: Life is a dream and dreams are life and we should be careful how we behave in either. <br /><br /> 	 Cliched but present: Forgiveness  leads to forgiveness (while bile to bile). <br /><br /> 	 A feminist aspect: Rosaura used to be the betrothed of Astolfo, a pretendent to the throne, during his time in Muscovy and before his arrival to Poland. The first scene opens with her finding Sigismund in his prison, as she seeks entrance to the Polish court to avenge herself on Astolfo, and she remains the second most important character of the play if not the most important. She is determined, resourceful, will dress as a man if this helps her cause, but will ask a man for help too; she is cunning and self-sufficient. An unexpectedly strong character I enjoyed as a counterpoint to Sigismund's weakness and volatility. <br /> <br /><br />Overall, an enjoyable take on the duality of the dream world and the waking world, with much plot besides.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10075334369922515169" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3020737715" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3020737715" />
    <a title="Shem the Penman" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/43331892-shem-the-penman"><img alt="Shem the Penman" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1493163225p2/43331892.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3020737715?book_show_action=true">Oct 24, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Shem the Penman" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Shem the Penman" href="/user/show/43331892-shem-the-penman">Shem the Penman</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780143104827" href="/book/show/42213.Life_Is_a_Dream">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3020737715" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6852505578010425746">Here is another first for me: my first dive into the wild and horrifying world of Spanish Golden Age drama, brought to me by the most famous play of the most renowned poet of probably the most prodigious century for commercial theatre in world history. After putting it off for years, I finally read Pedro Calderon de la Barca's <i>Life is a Dream</i>, translated from the Spanish into elegant rhymed pentameter by Dr. Gregary J. Racz. Coming in to this, I have a general familiarity with the course of earl</span>
  <span id="freeText6852505578010425746" style="display:none">Here is another first for me: my first dive into the wild and horrifying world of Spanish Golden Age drama, brought to me by the most famous play of the most renowned poet of probably the most prodigious century for commercial theatre in world history. After putting it off for years, I finally read Pedro Calderon de la Barca's <i>Life is a Dream</i>, translated from the Spanish into elegant rhymed pentameter by Dr. Gregary J. Racz. Coming in to this, I have a general familiarity with the course of early modern Spanish history: its manic, genocidal expansion of itself into the lands of the Indigenous people of the Americas and its economy's subsequent over-reliance on gold and silver imports in contrast to the paucity of the peninsula's agricultural resources; the zealous and bloodthirsty form of national Catholicism instituted by Ferdinand and Isabella in the wake of the Reconquista; and the ethnic cleansing of the peninsula's Jewish and Muslim inhabitants, whose aftershocks in providing a system of religious heredity provided one of the earliest examples in Europe of the notion of hereditary race. Despite this, I possess a very small amount of knowledge beyond my love of Cervantes and Velazquez as to the actual character and conditions of Spanish art and literature during this period. Nevertheless, let's see how far this Goodreads review can go!<br /><br />The plot pivots partly around one of my favourite 17th century concerns: the repercussions brought about by the interruption of divinely-sanctioned primogeniture succession and the subsequent tenuous line between the unsustainability of absolute monarchy and the horrors of civil war. In the play, the astrologically obsessed King Basilio of Poland reads in "the fatal confluence of stars" and in the dreams of his wife Queen Clorilene that his future son and sole heir to the throne shall be born a monster, "the most rebellious man/ The world could know, the cruelest prince / And even most ungodly king / Whose reckless rule would leave the realm / Divided and in open rift", and who would one day kill his father. As these signs seem to get all but confirmed when Clorilene dies in childbirth, Basilio chooses to prevent this future parricide by locking the infant prince Segismundo in a tower deep in the wilds of Poland for the rest of his days, and severed from all human contact save for the abusive tutelage of Basilio's advisor Clotaldo and his guards. Now, with the young prince having grown into adulthood while being completely cut off from external reality and whose existence has been actively suppressed by the state, Basilio decides to try out a little scientific experiment: bring Segismundo back to crown and crown him to see whether or not human beings can act outside the bounds of their star-decided fate and exercise free will. Meanwhile, the calculating and seductive Astolfo, Duke of Moscow and nephew of the king, jockeys for succession to the Polish throne while courting the king's niece Estrella. Thus, as in so much of Renaissance theatre, the concerns of politics, metaphysics, theology, science, personal relationships, and sheer human drama all interweave into each other to form a complex philosophical tapestry that is both captivating and unnerving. <br /><br />What contributes to Calderon's eponymously dreamlike mosaic is the form in which he had to work. Unlike the playhouses and courts that housed the Lord Chamberlain's and Lord Admiral's Men an ocean away in London, Calderon's productions were first performed outdoors in large, open courtyards known as corrales, curtainless and reliant on natural light. In these circumstances, even more austere than the cost-cutting companies on the south bank of the Thames, the reality of the play's world is extremely dependent on its construction and evocation on the language brought forth in the script and the bodies of the actors performing it, who each collaborate with the audience in building an expansive yet invisible space held together by collective imagination. (This model of mimetic collectivity, of course, has vast political implications throughout the Renaissance both in its rupture with the organic collectivity envisioned by natural law and in providing a model of politics itself as a form of stagecraft, with political agents adopting the roles of either tragic heroes or the playwrights themselves.) Art becomes a mirror for Nature to reflect its own self back at it. <br /><br />The aesthetic effect of this formal quality is that space-as-such becomes collapsed into the mind and language of the traversing body, which unravels scene after scene like a magician pulling handkerchiefs out of hat. This is shown in the opening scene, where the disgraced Russian noblewoman Rosaura and her fool Clarion first discover Segismundo in his tower in the wilderness: first we find Rosaura fallen off her horse and high on a rugged mountainside, then they see a palace downward into the hills, and they climb down, knock on the door, enter the palace, and find the enchained prince, who erupts into the dialogue like a pillar of smoke from an enchanted cauldron. This vertical and horizontal movement is so beautifully simple and magical, in which every single point of the landscape and, eventually, the country is reduced to the flat plane of the stage, which expands and contracts to contain within itself every inch of the play's cosmos. In the most pure distillation of this theatrical effect as seen in Marlowe's history plays, the stage becomes charged with the spectacular density brought about by representing both everywhere at once and yet nowhere in particular, the one unifying element being the will and consciousness of the theatrical personality. (Notice how the dramatis personae in <i>Edward II</i>, <i>Tamburlaine</i> seem to mostly pool and scatter together in shoals that break either upon each other or on the protagonist, and contrast this with the dramatic, clean scene transitions to be found in Shakespeare. The former is monistic, the latter pluralistic.) <br /><br />While there are certainly <i>places</i>, and very striking ones, in Calderon's play such as Segismundo's prison and the Polish court, every moment of stable mimesis in such locations is broken by the horrifying interruptions of its protagonist, who in each act makes a seismic transformation into a different kind of person living in a different kind of dream. Both of the central characters--Segismundo and Rosaura--seem to end up in roles at the end that are simultaneously predictable in ideological terms while being completely unexpected on a dramatic level. In the second act, Segismundo predictably wreaks havoc on the court, demanding a narcissistic degree of obedience to every arbitrary order he makes with a subsequently violent bout of rage when it is not instantly carried out. He justifies his tyranny as an act of retribution against what has long been stolen from him, and once his fate has supposedly been confirmed Basilio has him drugged, carted back to his prison, and told that everything he had experienced in the court had been a dream. His solipsistic vengeance provides justification for his continued imprisonment in solipsism. <br /><br />But his return from exile, while hid from him, cannot be hid by the people, who along with Rosaura break into his prison and pledge to restore him against the claims of the foreign Duke Astolfo. But being enlightened as to his previous position proves to be of comfort to Segismundo, who becomes caught in the typical Renaissance terror of not being able to tell a true dream from a false one; in the end, it makes no difference, and Segismundo simply dreams himself into a position of enlightened monarch in much the same way he dreamed himself into the position of avenging tyrant. When he is victorious and his succession is ensured, all the potential subversion which his act of rebellion would have embodied is wrapped up, empowering his own class while suppressing the strength of the lower class that had put him in power. <br /><br />The reality of the Renaissance theatre as an ideological state apparatus is one that cannot be ignored, either in the Spanish, French, or English contexts. Nevertheless, one of the greatest benefits to this period's artistic longevity is the way in which its surfeit of metaphysical, poetic, and political concerns cut across and weave into each other to create, if not a revolutionary content, then at least a form of recuperation which is ultimately doomed to failure while reflecting the contradictory demands of politics and metaphysics. </span>
  <a data-text-id="6852505578010425746" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer12799578904817913466">*read in parallel the original version by Calderón de la Barca and the English translation provided by <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/1804382.Michael_Kidd' title='Michael Kidd' rel='nofollow noopener'>Michael Kidd</a><br/>*the quotes will be in English<br/><br/><i>Given that life is so short, let's dream, my soul, let's dream!</i><br/>Play about the choices we have in life, the good and the bad in it, destiny, change, honour, present.   <span id="freeText12799578904817913466" style="display:none">*read in parallel the original version by Calderón de la Barca and the English translation provided by <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/1804382.Michael_Kidd" title="Michael Kidd" rel="nofollow noopener">Michael Kidd</a><br />*the quotes will be in English<br /><br /><i>Given that life is so short, let's dream, my soul, let's dream!</i><br />Play about the choices we have in life, the good and the bad in it, destiny, change, honour, present. It lit in me the flame of fighting and of living. <br /><br /><i>As a woman, I come to move you to the cause of my honour; as a man, I come to encourage you to recover your crown. As woman, I come to beg your sympathy by throwing myself at your feet;; as a man, I come to serve you by aiding your soldiers. As a woman, I come to request your support in my dishonor and anguish; as a man, I come to support you with my sword and character. And finally, consider that if you try to seduce me today as a woman, as a man I will slay you in legitimate defense of my honour; for in restoring my good name I must act as a lovesick woman to plead for help and as a man to win respect.</i> - Rosaura<br />I found this passage to be an evidence to gender fluidity in the play (Rosaura also pretended to be a man in the beginning) that could also be read in a feminist matter. The double-standards are exposed, the ideas that only men could win their honour back and fight and that women can only make themselves miserable and <b>beg</b> for honour and pardon are fought.<br /><br /><br /><u>Favourite character</u>: Rosaura<br /><u>Favourite quote</u>: <i>The rich man dreams of more riches, which only bring him more worries; the poor man dreams that he suffers in misery and poverty; the man who improves his lot dreams; the man who toils and petitions dreams; the man insults and offends dreams. And in the world, in short, everyone dreams who he is although no one realizes it. I dream that I'm here, weighed down by these chains, and I've dreamt that I found myself in more flattering circumstances. What is life? A frenzy. What is life? A vain hope, a shadow, a fiction. The greatest good is fleeting, for all life is a dream and even dreams are but dreams.</i><br /><u>Favourite scene</u>: the beginning of the play, when Rosaura finds Segismundo chained<br /><br />An amazing read! </span>

  <span id="freeText16801814677706650459" style="display:none">From the back cover: Astrological omens predict that if King Basilio's son Segismundo is crowned, he will become a horrible tyrant who will bring destruction to his kingdom. Basilio imprisons Segismundo for life, but decades later he decides to let his son prove his ability to defy the stars. Allowed to rule the palace, Segismundo wreaks bloody vengeance on the kingdom, confirming the prediction of the stars, and the prince is returned to his prison. In Nilo Cruz's sublime translation of Pedro Calderón de la Barca's classic, the question of whether life is a dream or an illusion takes on a renewed relevance and urgency.<br /><br />I have read this several times, and this is the Samuel French, Nilo Cruz edition. I tried to add it as a book, but...it wouldn't let me...anyway, this was on my readings list for the fall 2014 SUNY Sullivan play, and almost was chosen, and I'm excited to say that I added it again, and it was chosen, so I'm very excited to tackle this classic for our 2015-2016 Season. This was a favorite of my prison students at Washington State Penitentiary, when I taught Representative Plays, and, I think, I am going to try a prison concept...it should be fun...</span>

<div id="review_22804529" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/22804529" />
    <a title="Julie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/207567-julie"><img alt="Julie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1184948404p2/207567.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/22804529?book_show_action=true">May 23, 2008</a>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('ZVC1L2MfHKC74JhJkW2QwYmXLTiJ+2cV2i4x9X8ZNameW0oW8LjZzEtV/RFBoxIbn+44NHwAl3p7C5Pvyqrg/w==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('C89baUwDdXcMg2V6TMqoPqdaZwNZdX7ca9l7X9vgdabwxKRQ36SwG/w2ACKcBCrksSNyD6yOjrPK/NlFblOg8A==')})
  }
//]]>

La ficción deja aquí de ser un puro vehículo narrativo, transmisor de historias, para convertirse en un universo textual de fecundas sugerencias. El título, Niebla, expresa con claridad el propósito novelesco de desdibujar lo visible y materializar, en cambio, lo impalpable. En este ambiente vemos moverse a un hombre esencialmente frustrado, Augusto Pérez, sobre cuya muerte nos vemos obligados a pronunciarnos. Germán Gullón, reconocido como uno de los primeros especialistas en novela contemporánea, facilita en esta edición una pauta de lectura que conduce a una riqueza de comprensión insospechada de la novela en sí y de cuanto supone, como cima, en el proceso de la narrativa española.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"771068652109501626\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/227771.Pedro_Calder_n_de_la_Barca">About Pedro Calderón de la Barca</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>

<a href="/author/show/227771.Pedro_Calder_n_de_la_Barca"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1227042355p3/227771.jpg);'></div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/227771.Pedro_Calder_n_de_la_Barca">Pedro Calderón de la Barca

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer2349590298702516982">Pedro Calderón de la Barca y Henao was a dramatist of the Spanish Golden Age.<br/><br/>Calderón initiated what has been called the second cycle of Spanish Golden Age theatre. Whereas his predecessor, Lope de Vega, pioneered the dramatic forms and genres of Spanish Golden Age theatre, Calderón polished and perfected them. Whereas Lope's strength lay in the sponteneity and naturalness of his work, Calderón's </span>
  <span id="freeText2349590298702516982" style="display:none">Pedro Calderón de la Barca y Henao was a dramatist of the Spanish Golden Age.<br /><br />Calderón initiated what has been called the second cycle of Spanish Golden Age theatre. Whereas his predecessor, Lope de Vega, pioneered the dramatic forms and genres of Spanish Golden Age theatre, Calderón polished and perfected them. Whereas Lope's strength lay in the sponteneity and naturalness of his work, Calderón's strength lay in his capacity for poetic beauty, dramatic structure and philosophical depth. Calderón was a perfectionist who often revisited and reworked his plays, even long after they debuted. This perfectionism was not just limited to his own work: many of his plays rework existing plays or scenes by other dramatists, improving their depth, complexity, and unity. (Many European playwrights of the time, such as Molière, Corneille and Shakespeare, reworked old plays in this way.) Calderón excelled above all others in the genre of the "auto sacramental", in which he showed a seemingly inexhaustible capacity to giving new dramatic forms to a given set of theological constructs. Calderón wrote 120 "comedias", 80 "autos sacramentales" and 20 short comedic works called "entremeses"</span>
  <a data-text-id="2349590298702516982" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>
